                .addSize([320, 100], [[300, 50], [320, 50]])

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201600845')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="dfab3ccdb658449894bde50126b42fed"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="dfab3ccdb658449894bde50126b42fed">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '98804174-424f-497b-b74c-857e5b53a743'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '54CA23D8073DE1361789B0EABD19C779';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d62915230" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>As the delegate of tunnel structure sodium titanates, Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>6</sub>O<sub>13</sub> nanorods with dominant large interlayer spacing exposed facet are prepared. The exposed large interlayers provide facile channels for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction when this material is used as anode for Na-ion batteries (NIBs). After an activation process, this NIB anode achieves a high specific capacity (a capacity of 172 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and outstanding cycling stability (a capacity of 109 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2800 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), showing its promising application on large-scale energy storage systems. Furthermore, the electrochemical and structural characterization reveals that the expanded interlayer spacings should be in charge of the activation process, including the enhanced kinetics, the lowered apparent activation energy, and the increased capacity.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201600845-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201600845-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>In order to satisfy the increasing demand for clean and sustainable energy sources, large-scale energy storage systems with good reliability, high power, low cost, environmental benignity, and safety are critically needed.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0001_R_d62915222e379" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Science Chem. Soc. Rev. Sci. Chin. Mater.">1</a></span> Na-ion batteries (NIBs) are considered as potential alternatives because of the low cost and wide availability of Na.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0002_R_d62915222e381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Chem. Commun.">2</a></span> Many materials or analogues used for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) anodes, such as graphite, Sn, and Na<sub>4</sub>Ti<sub>5</sub>O<sub>12</sub> etc. have been prepared and tested as NIBs anodes. However, the fact that the reported capacities and cycling stabilities are far from our anticipations, an important factor to this situation is that the ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) is larger than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å), which causes sluggish solid-state diffusion and results in the materials that suitable for LIBs are always not fit for NIBs.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0004_R_d62915222e394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Mater. Chem. A">4</a></span> Conversely, channels that oversized for Li<sup>+</sup> may be well fit for Na<sup>+</sup> to transfer or located in, as reported for FeF<sub>3</sub>·0.5 H<sub>2</sub>O polytype and prussian blue analogues with interesting tunnels.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0005_R_d62915222e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Am. Chem. Soc. Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun.">5</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Sodium titanates (Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<i><sub>n</sub></i>O<sub>2</sub><i><sub>n</sub></i><sub>+1</sub>, 2 ≤ <i>n</i> ≤ 9) are widely employed in many practical applications owing to their high ionic exchange ability.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0006_R_d62915222e422" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Lett.">6</a></span> Generally speaking, titanates with higher sodium content are formed by the chain-arranged TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedras and show layered structures, such as Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>;<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0007_R_d62915222e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0008_R_d62915222e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A Chem. Mater.">8</a></span> while the titanates with lower sodium content are always tunnel structures, such as Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>6</sub>O<sub>13</sub> (NTO).<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0009_R_d62915222e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">9</a>-<a href="#smll201600845-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0011_R_d62915222e441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Commun.">11</a></span> In NTO, a 3D framework with two different sizes of tunnels is formed by the units of every three edge-shared TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedras. The sizes of the continuous tunnels are larger than the ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup>. Thus, the tunnels could serve as excellent host structures for fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. The unique 3D open framework is so stable that could accommodate volume changes and avoid structural degradation during cycling, which is in favor of achieving long cyclic stability.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0012_R_d62915222e450" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Environ. Sci.">12</a></span> Therefore, NTO could be a good candidate for NIBs with long cyclic stability and good rate capability. Several pioneering works have proved the Na storage property of this material.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0011_R_d62915222e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0013_R_d62915222e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Inorg. Chem.">13</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0014_R_d62915222e452" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">14</a></span> Unfortunately, the fact is that the capacities are still low, and the rate capabilities are still unsatisfied. The main reason is that the synthesized materials are lacking a facile exposed channel for Na<sup>+</sup> transfer, thus the merits of the tunnel structure are stifled. The works of Wang and Fan on vanadium oxide suggest a large interlayer spacing is facile for ions transfer and that could be dominantly exposed by preferred.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0015_R_d62915222e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0016_R_d62915222e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a></span> In this context, the electrochemical performance of NTO could be improved by preparing a controlled facet with large interlayer spacings and making full use of the uniqueness of 3D open framework with tunnel structure.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Besides the crystalline structures could essentially affect the electrochemical performance of an electrode, the morphology also plays an important role in its performance. As evidenced by the equation 
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201600845-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/f3cad984-cb6f-449f-91ff-e017651c67da/smll201600845-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>The ions diffusion time <img alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0002" loading="lazy" class="section_image" src="/cms/asset/5988525d-6193-4400-88e4-b8f6b995d12b/smll201600845-math-0002.png"> could be shorted by reducing the dimensions (<i>L</i>) of the active materials, which largely affects the rate capability of an electrode. Moreover, a nanosized electrode always owns large surface, which gives birth to more active sites to take part in electrochemical activity, resulting an enlarged capacity. Therefore, energy-storage materials with 1D nanostructure, such as nanowires, nanotubes, and nanorods, are always the focuses of researchers owing to their shortened ions transport distance, large surface-to-volume ratio, and innate structural stability.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0017_R_d62915222e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">17</a></span></div>
         
         <p>In this work, 1D high-stable NTO nanorods, as the delegate of 3D tunnel structure sodium titanates, were obtained by annealing the ultra-long hydrated sodium titanates nanowires precursor. Different from the relatively smaller interlayer spacing of the exposed facets in previous works,<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0011_R_d62915222e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Commun.">11</a></span> these nanorods have dominant exposed facet (−2 0 1) with large interlayer spacing (6.27 Å), which are beneficial to the Na ions insertion and extraction. When invested as anode of NIBs anode, outstanding cycling stability (a capacity of 109 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2800 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and high specific capacity (a capacity of 172 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles) were delivered by this material after an activation process, showing a competitive application on large-scale energy storage ststems. Furtuermore, we confirmed the solid solution Na storage mechanism on this electrode using Synchrotron X-ray powder diffraction (SXRPD) experiments, transmission electron microscopy (TEM), and ex situ X-ray diffraction (XRD). The expanded interlayer spacings during cycling should be responsible for the activation process, which triggered the kinetics, lowered the apparent activation energy (from 70.30 to 64.82 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>), and increased the capacity.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201600845-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201600845-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201600845-sec-0030">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201600845-sec-0030-title">2.1 Formation Mechanism and Structure Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The general formation process of NTO nanorods is illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0001">1</a> based on the scanning electron microscope (SEM) and TEM images (Figures S1 and S2, Supporting Information). Firstly, low crystallization TiO<sub>2</sub> particles (Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0001">1</a>a) were obtained by the slow hydrolysis of tetrabutyl titanate (TBOT). When these particles were treated in NaOH solution (1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span>), a dissolution-recrystallization precession occurred and hydrated sodium titanate nanowires were formed ultimately. As illustrated by Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0001">1</a>b–d and Figure S1 in the Supporting Information (SEM images for the precursors at 1, 3, 6, 9 h hydrothermal durations, Supporting Information), the morphology evolution experiences a process that from particles to nanosheets, then to nanorods, and further evaluated to ultra-long nanowires that consisted of many paralleled nanotubes (confirmed by Figures S2b and f, Supporting Information). This phenomenon is in accordance with Tang et al.'s work.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0018_R_d62915222e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Mater.">18</a></span> The crystallinity evolution of TiO<sub>2</sub> particles to hydrated sodium titanate nanowires was confirmed by the XRD and Raman spectra (Figure S3, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0019_R_d62915222e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Electrochim. Acta">19</a></span> When the ultra-long hydrated sodium titanate nanowires were annealed at 700 °C for 10 h, NTO nanorods were obtained owing to the temperature gradient existing along the axial direction (as shown in Figure S2, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0020_R_d62915222e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201600845-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d3b69127-f970-43fa-81b5-72d99f3ef859/smll201600845-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d3b69127-f970-43fa-81b5-72d99f3ef859/smll201600845-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/851080c4-70f1-444e-b693-8d8f46bbb644/smll201600845-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d3b69127-f970-43fa-81b5-72d99f3ef859/smll201600845-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600845-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600845" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematics of the formation of NTO nanorods and the corresponding SEM images on different stages. a) The hydrolysis product of TBOT; b–d) the hydrated sodium titanate at different hydrothermal durations: b) 1 h, c) 9 h, d) 12 h, e) the annealed product NTO.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0002">2</a>a shows the refinement XRD pattern of the as-prepared NTO nanorods. The analysis suggests no impurity in the product. All the diffraction peaks can be well indexed to monoclinic NTO with space group C2/m (JCPDS No. 73–1369), as suggested by Figure S4a in the Supporting Information. The crystal structure is presented by Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0002">2</a>b. It can be seen that every three edge-shared TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedras acts as the component unit of the tunnel structure with 3D framework. The units share corners in <i>c</i> axis to form zigzag layers, arhombic tunnels with side length about 3.0 Å are left and resemble that of anatase TiO<sub>2</sub>. The zigzag layers share corners in <i>a</i> axis to form other big quasi-rectangular tunnels with size of 8.4 Å × 2.7 Å and two Na atoms are held.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0014_R_d62915222e561" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">14</a></span> What worthy mentioned is that anatase TiO<sub>2</sub> is a good host for Na<sup>+</sup>, as evidenced by previous works.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0021_R_d62915222e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces Nano Lett. Energy Environ. Sci. Chem. Commun. Chem. Mater.">21</a></span> Moreover, the open 3D framework is critical to develop a satisfactory electrode with long cyclic stability owing to its high ability to accommodate crystal structure distortion. The NTO nanorods were further characterized by Raman spectrum and Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra. In the Raman spectrum (Figure S4b, Supporting Information), the intense peaks below 400 cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to Na<span class="icomoon"></span>O band vibrations and Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O stretching vibrations. Typically, the peaks appeared at 130 and 676 cm<sup>−1</sup> are corresponded to the formation of Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti bonds, the peak located at 880 cm<sup>−1</sup> is assigned to the short Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O stretching vibration with low coordination, and the peaks at 195 and 276 cm<sup>−1</sup> are the characteristic of Na<span class="icomoon"></span>Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0010_R_d62915222e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Raman Spectrosc.">10</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0022_R_d62915222e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></span> In terms of FT-IR spectra (Figure S4c, Supporting Information), apart from the peaks located at 3410 and 1640 cm<sup>−1</sup> which ascribed to physisorption water, the peaks at 1390, 1065, and 890 cm<sup>−1</sup> are associated with the alkali–oxygen vibrations and that at 470 cm<sup>−1</sup> is attributed to the vibrations of Ti<span class="icomoon"></span>O bonds in TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0007_R_d62915222e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0022_R_d62915222e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201600845-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/23a3b5b0-ccb6-4217-a7e3-bf8c2df47dd3/smll201600845-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/23a3b5b0-ccb6-4217-a7e3-bf8c2df47dd3/smll201600845-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/12ad2d56-3e76-46c4-862f-8fe38f6e78fd/smll201600845-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/23a3b5b0-ccb6-4217-a7e3-bf8c2df47dd3/smll201600845-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600845-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600845" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The refinement XRD pattern a) of the obtained Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>6</sub>O<sub>13</sub> sample and b) its crystal structure.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The detailed microstructure of the NTO nanorods is characterized by TEM. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0003">3</a>a, the nanorods are well dispersed without aggregation and their dimensions are homogeneous with the width about 40–80 nm and the length are in the range of 0.8–1.0 μm. The high-resolution TEM (HR-TEM) image in Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0003">3</a>b shows that the tubular structure of the precursor totally disappeared and suggests the dominated exposed facets (−2 0 1). Upon inspection of HR-TEM image in Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0003">3</a>c, the interlayer spacings of 6.27 and 2.84 Å are corresponded to the (−2 0 1) and (−1 1 2) planes of monoclinic NTO, respectively. Furthermore, an interplanar angle about 60.8° between these two planes is detected, confirming the pure crystalline phase of the NTO nanorods (the interlayer spacing of the planes and their interplanar angles are summarized in Table S1, Supporting Information). The dominant exposed facets (−2 0 1) and large interlayer spacing (6.27 Å) could offer channels for Na<sup>+</sup> transferring facilely, which is important in boosting the rate capability.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0015_R_d62915222e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - ACS Nano">15</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0016_R_d62915222e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201600845-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f1834908-f439-4a71-8e94-69adfcb4cffb/smll201600845-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f1834908-f439-4a71-8e94-69adfcb4cffb/smll201600845-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f0f70233-d803-4c31-bdf4-8db2445baf2e/smll201600845-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f1834908-f439-4a71-8e94-69adfcb4cffb/smll201600845-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600845-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600845" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) TEM and b,c) HR-TEM images of NTO nanorods with b) a single nanorod and c) the corresponding fast Fourier transform (FFT) pattern in set.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201600845-sec-0040">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201600845-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance and Na Storage Mechanism</h3>
            
            <p>As for the unique structure of the NTO nanorods, superior electrochemical performances of this material for Na storage are expected. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0004">4</a>a, this electrode delivers a specific capacity of 172 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 300 cycles and 129 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 800 cycles, showing a largely improved capacity and long cyclic stability (the comparative results are listed in Table S2, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0011_R_d62915222e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0013_R_d62915222e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Inorg. Chem.">13</a></span> The rate capability is also excellent, as depicted in Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0004">4</a>b. After an activation process, remarkably stable and high capacities of 160, 145, 133, 123, and 119 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, and 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> (average of discharge capacities for each 30 cycles) were obtained, respectively, and then recovered to 174 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Importantly, after cycled at different current densities, a capacity of 120 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> is delivered when this electrode further cycled at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 90.8% (109 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) of that is retained even after 2800 cycles with a coulombic efficiency above 99%. Moreover, the SEM image of this electrode after such aggressive cycles shows that the original morphology is well preserved and without any aggregation (Figure S5, Supporting Information), indicating the highly stable structure of this electrode. Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0004">4</a>c shows the discharge–charge curves at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> between 2.5 and 0.01 V. Obviously, the plateaus at 0.91 V for charge and 0.80 V for discharge undergo a process of getting longer before being submerged by the increasing plateaus at 0.53 and 0.37 V, exhibiting a capacity increasing. The CV curves confirmed this phenomenon, as depicted in Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0004">4</a>d. The position of the high-voltage peaks (0.91 and 0.80 V) does not change while the peak intensity increases as the cycles going, indicating easier insertion–extraction process of Na<sup>+</sup>. Moreover, the dominant role of the high-voltage peaks (0.91 and 0.80 V) is taken placed by the low-voltage peaks (0.53 and 0.37 V), which becomes more and more intense and broad. On the other hand, the cathodic peak shifts higher voltage and stabilizes at 0.37 V, corresponding to a lowered overpotential. The lowered overpotential and increased peak intensity suggest the improved electron and ion transport kinetics.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0016_R_d62915222e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Lett.">16</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0017_R_d62915222e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">17</a></span> Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction at low-voltage (0.37 and 0.53 V) is beneficial to raise their energy density and specific energy when used as anode material.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0022_R_d62915222e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a></span> Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is a useful tool for identifying the relationship between kinetics and the electrochemical performance of an electrode. Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0004">4</a>e presents the Nyquist plots of the NTO nanorods electrode at different stages in frequencies from 100 kHz to 0.1 Hz. These plots share the same feature of a depressed semicircle (at high frequency) and a linear part (at low frequency). The size of the semicircular is related to the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) in an electrode reaction, and the inclined line indicates the Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) associated with ion diffusion in the solid.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0022_R_d62915222e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Environ. Sci.">22</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0023_R_d62915222e713" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Nano">23</a></span> The smaller and smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> confirms that the Na reaction kinetics is improving along with the cycling. Obviously, the activation process with an enhanced kinetics does exist in our electrode and this phenomenon has not been reported in pioneer works about NTO electrodes, no matter in NIBs or LIBs.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0011_R_d62915222e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0013_R_d62915222e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Inorg. Chem.">13</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0014_R_d62915222e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">14</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0024_R_d62915222e719" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Electrochem. Commun. J. Power Sources">24</a></span> Thus, it is meaningful to explore the reason for this issue.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201600845-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7feb42a3-8495-4e91-bafb-87ceaecc1552/smll201600845-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/7feb42a3-8495-4e91-bafb-87ceaecc1552/smll201600845-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f64df41a-8d16-4581-83fd-a534f41b80b6/smll201600845-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7feb42a3-8495-4e91-bafb-87ceaecc1552/smll201600845-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600845-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600845" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of NTO nanorods anode. a) Cycling performance at 0.1 and 0.4 A g<sup>−1</sup>; b) rate performance at different current densities from 0.1 to 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; c) discharge–charge curves of various cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>; d) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>; and e) EIS plots from 100k to 0.1 Hz at different cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The Na storage mechanism of our NTO nanorods electrode was investigated by SXRPD experiment (the details can be seen in Experimental Section). Figure S6 in the Supporting Information shows the SXRPD pattern of the assembled coin cell at open circuit voltage (OCV). Due to the reflections of NTO are significantly weaker than that of Cu (current collector) and Na (anode), which increased the difficulty of further analysis, we performed single peak-fitting to examine the phase evolution of NTO during discharging. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0005">5</a>a and Figure S7 in the Supporting Information, the (2 0 0), (0 2 0), and (1 1 0) reflections move continuously during initial Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and then remain at the same position at ≈0.2 V. The continuous moving of the reflection with intensity decreasing indicates the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion process of NTO at 0.8 V plateau proceeds through a solid–solution reaction to form sodium-rich NTO. On the other hand, the reflection position remained nearly unchanged, alongside with the increase in peak width and decrease in peak intensity, at the 0.2 V plateau (this also can be seen in CV curves, Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0004">4</a>d), suggesting two possibilities: 
               <ol start="1" class="">
                  <li>The NTO phase underwent a two-phase reaction, where the new phase is in amorphous that cannot be observed clearly in the operando patterns.</li>
                  <li>The solid–solution reaction continues and the continuous sodium intake induced structure re-arrangement inside the monoclinic phase.</li>
               </ol>
            </div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201600845-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/24331078-0f31-430c-8ea2-da4e49808b02/smll201600845-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/24331078-0f31-430c-8ea2-da4e49808b02/smll201600845-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/21eb731e-4d22-4932-915e-bedbcaa329bb/smll201600845-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/24331078-0f31-430c-8ea2-da4e49808b02/smll201600845-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600845-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600845" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Contour plots showing the changes in peak position, peak width, peak intensity alongside with the discharge curves. b–e) HR-TEM images of the cycled NTO nanorods at different states: b) the third fully discharge and c) charge state, d) the 30<sup>th</sup> and e) 60<sup>th</sup> cycles at fully charge state.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">However, the TEM images (Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0005">5</a>b–e and Figure S8, Supporting Information) after different cycles suggest the well crystallinity of the electrode and no amorphous structure can be seen in the body of the NTO nanorods except the solid-electrolyte-interphase (SEI) layers on the surface and the distorted crystal lattices in some regions (Figure S8, Supporting Information). Therefore, the solid–solution reaction continues at 0.2 V and the further sodium intake does not affect the structure orderliness, showing its original XRD patterns (Figure S9, Supporting Information) besides the peak shift of (−2 0 1). The overall Na<sup>+</sup> insertion–extraction process occurring in this NTO electrode could be elaborated as follows 
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201600845-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/605d2f95-a5f6-492a-9817-855ba843f7a8/smll201600845-math-0003.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0003" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0003" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>x</i> is the mole fraction of inserted Na ions, and that is considered as a solid–solution Na storage mechanism. This result further confirms the suggestions of ex situ and in situ XRD performed by the pioneer works,<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0011_R_d62915222e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Commun.">11</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0013_R_d62915222e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Inorg. Chem.">13</a></span> though their electrodes with limited capacity or inferior cyclic stability.</div>
            
            <p>As important features of our NTO nanorods NIBs anode, the increased capacity and largely enhanced kinetics are impressive and the origin for this activation process is interesting. The increased capacity and enhanced kinetics are brought by the nanosize effect on conversion mechanism electrodes,<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0025_R_d62915222e802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Mater. Adv. Sci.">25</a></span> which accompany a reconstructive nanostructure and enlarged electrode–electrolyte contract area. Different from them, this solid solution storage mechanism electrode keeps its original morphology and nanostructure. When the crystal structure of NTO is observed in <i>c</i> axis, zigzag chains are formed by the arrays of every three edge-shared TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedrals which share corners and two different sizes of tunnels were left by them. Therefore, the structure of NTO could be regarded as “layered” at some level and the ions insertion–extraction into the tunnels may cause the layer distorted. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0005">5</a>b–e, the interlayer spacing of (−2 0 1) keeps increase before stabilized about 7.1 Å. Some domains of this electrode show distortion after 100 cycles, as shown in Figure S8c in the Supporting Information. The expanded interlayer spacings were resulted from the repetition of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction, conversely, the large space facilitates the migration of Na<sup>+</sup>, lower energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0026_R_d62915222e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">26</a>-<a href="#smll201600845-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0028_R_d62915222e817" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Commun.">28</a></span></p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Base on the relationship between Arrhenius equation (Equation <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll201600845-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a>) and the exchange current (<i>i</i><sup>0</sup>),<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0028_R_d62915222e826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Commun.">28</a>, <a href="#smll201600845-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0029_R_d62915222e826" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">29</a></span> apparent activation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) could be calculated as follows 
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201600845-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/b5554b1b-1c49-4e7e-9673-5be3999c1961/smll201600845-math-0004.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0004" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0004" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201600845-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/f5b7d8d5-aa46-4f80-9068-1934a0d92b91/smll201600845-math-0005.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0005" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0005" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">Where <i>R</i> is the gas constant, <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, <i>n</i> is the number of transferred electrons, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant, and <i>A</i> is a temperature independent coefficient. The equations can be rearranged to 
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201600845-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/7815f6de-5bae-4add-80bb-863d56d414fd/smll201600845-math-0006.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0006" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0006" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201600845-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/748921e5-a239-4b8e-9ec2-80324208d7e8/smll201600845-math-0007.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0007" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0007" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(6)</span></div>
               <div class="inline-equation" id="smll201600845-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/94f54ef1-ab98-4753-9ad1-843960138a3e/smll201600845-math-0008.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0008" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201600845:smll201600845-math-0008" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
            </div>
            
            <p>EIS of the pristine and cycled NTO nanorods anodes were carried out at different temperatures (Figure S10, Supporting Information), and the values of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and ln(<i>i</i><sup>0</sup>) are summarized in Table S3 in the Supporting Information. Arrhenius plots of ln(<i>i</i><sup>0</sup>) as a function of 1/<i>T</i> of them are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll201600845-fig-0006">6</a>. Based on the fitting results, the apparent activation energies for the pristine and cycled NTO nanorods anodes are 70.30 and 64.82 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Thus, the insertion and extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> becomes easier after the interlayer spacings being enlarged. This phenomenon is also found in systems of layered structure materials, such as MoS<sub>2</sub>, in which the transport kinetics of Li<sup>+</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> are enhanced by the enlarged interlayer spacings.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0026_R_d62915222e892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">26</a></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201600845-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d25cc46f-2dbe-46b4-9138-6d1aceb3cc58/smll201600845-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/d25cc46f-2dbe-46b4-9138-6d1aceb3cc58/smll201600845-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5386520c-34d4-4f8b-95af-620269f8cfd9/smll201600845-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d25cc46f-2dbe-46b4-9138-6d1aceb3cc58/smll201600845-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600845-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600845" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Arrhenius plots of ln(<i>i</i><sup>0</sup>) versus 1/<i>T</i> for (1) cycled and (2) pristine NTO nanorods anodes.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The excellent performance of this NTO nanorods electrode can be attributed to its inherited 3D open framework structure with proper tunnel sizes and the exposed facets with large interlayer spacings. Firstly, the tunnels with proper sizes offer the located sites and migration channels for Na<sup>+</sup>. Secondly, the enlarged interlayer spacings during cycling facilitate the ions insertion–extraction and give birth to more active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, acting as the role of enhancing the kinetics, reducing the apparent activation energy, and increasing the capacity. Most importantly, the 3D framework of the tunnel structure is stable to hold the distorted lattice caused by enlarged interlayer spacings, ensuring the super-long cyclic stability of the electrode.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201600845-sec-0050">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201600845-sec-0050-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, highly stable NTO nanorods with dominant large interlayer spacing exposed facets were prepared by annealing ultra-long hydrated sodium titanates nanotubes precursor. The dominant large interlayer spacing exposed facets provid exposed channels for facile Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction. When used as NIBs anode, these NTO nanorods achieved an improved specific capacity, outstanding cycling stability, and rate capability (a capacity of 172 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 109 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2800 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>). The enlarged interlayer spacings should be in charge of the activation process. Importantly, the extraordinary electrochemical performance on Na storage makes this NTO nanorods electrode a promising material for large-scale energy storage systems.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201600845-sec-0060">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201600845-sec-0060-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p>The reagents were obtained from J&amp;K and used without further purification.</p>
         
         <p><i>Materials Synthesis</i>: High-stable NTO nanorods were prepared by a hydrothermal method and subsequent heat treatment. Typically, 1 mL TBOT was dissolved in 50 mL ethanol (EtOH) by ultrasonication, then 60 mL the mixture of H<sub>2</sub>O and EtOH (1:5, in volume) was dropped into the reaction solution and stirred to form a white emulsion. White powder (TiO<sub>2</sub> with low crystallization) was obtained after the emulsion washed by centrifugation with water and air-dried. The white powder (0.21 g) was well dispersed into 70 mL of NaOH solution (1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span>), and then transferred into 100 mL teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave. The autoclave was put in an oven with a roating speed of 10 r min<sup>−1</sup> at 200 °C for 12 h. After cooling down to room temperature, the product (hydrate sodium titanate, white) was collected by centrifugation, washed by H<sub>2</sub>O to reach a pH value ≈8 and dried in air. When this product was annealed at 700 °C for 10 h in air, NTO nanorods were obtained.</p>
         
         <p><i>Characterization of Materials</i>: The crystal structures of the materials were characterized by XRD (Rigaku MiniFlex II, with Cu Kα radiation, λ = 1.5408 Å). The morphology and microstructure were characterized by SEM (JEOL JSM-7500) and TEM (JEOL-2100F, 200 kV). Raman spectra were recorded on InVia Reflex with excitation of 532 nm.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: Electrochemical performance tests were carried out using a two-electrode cell with sodium metal as the counter and reference electrode. The anode was made of NTO, super-p carbon and sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) in a weight ratio of 75:15:10. The loading amount of the electrode material was measured ≈0.80 mg cm<sup>−2</sup> by a microbalance (Mettler, XS105DU) with an accuracy of 0.01 mg. Glass fiber and 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in polycarbonate (PC) and fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) (PC:FEC = 95:5, in volume) were used as separator and electrolyte. The cells were aged for 6 h before measurement. Cyclic voltammogram (CV) tests and EIS measurements both were performed on a CHI660b electrochemical workstation (Chenhua, Shanghai, China). The galvanostatic charge–discharge tests were carried out using a Land Battery Measurement system (Land CT2001A, China) at room temperature.</p>
         
         <p><i>SXRPD Measurement</i>: SXRPD experiment was conducted on the powder diffraction beamline at the Australian Synchrotron. A customized CR2032 coin cell with open windows for use in SXRPD experiments was made.<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0030_R_d62915222e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A J. Phys. Chem. C Sync. Rad. News">30</a></span> Electrodes were prepared by mixing the as-prepared NTO powder with carbon black (Super P, TIMCAL) and polyvinylidene fluoride (in a 80:10:10 weight ratio) in anhydrous <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (Sigma-Aldrich, 99.5%) to form a homogeneous slurry and then uniformly pasted onto Cu foil before being dried in a vacuum oven at 100 °C for 24 h and pressed prior to the assembly of the coin cell. The coin cell was assembled using sodium disks (Sigma-Aldrich) as the counter electrode, porous glass fiber (Millipore) as separator, and 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate as an electrolyte with 2 wt% fluorinated ethyl carbonate electrolyte additive. Holes for permitting synchrotron X-ray beam transmission were punched to form open windows on the top and bottom casing and then sealed with kapton tape and wax. The cell was galvanostatically discharged from OCV to 0.2 V (vs Na) at constant current of 0.0124 mA during data collection. SXRPD experiments were conducted on the powder diffraction beamline at the Australian Synchrotron where data were collected every 3.40 min during discharging. The lattice response of the monoclinic phase during cycle was extracted and calculated using a single peak-fitting analysis. Single peak-fitting was performed using the large-array manipulation program (LAMP) to track the changes in peak position,<span><a href="#smll201600845-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600845-bib-0031_R_d62915222e997" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Neutron Res.">31</a></span> intensity, and width, of the (200), (110), and (020) reflections of the monoclinic phase.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

      <section class="article-section article-section__references" id="article-references-section-1">
